from heretics preaching confusion.

Don't get close with prideful people,

those in love with small teachings,

those studying just to sound smart,

those breaking precepts behind closed doors.

Stay away from monks in name only,

from nuns who love to play around,

from laypeople deep in desires

seeking extinction for themselves.

If such people come to you

with good hearts and sincere questions,

wanting to hear the Buddha's Way,

then teach them without fear,

Teach them without hoping for reward,

without expecting praise or payment.

But don't seek them out,

don't make them your companions.

Stay away from widows and young women,

from those confused about their nature.

Don't get close with butchers and hunters,

with fishermen killing for money,

With people selling meat for a living,

selling bodies, selling shame.

Don't go near violent sports,

entertainments where people hurt each other,

Don't associate with prostitutes—

keep clear of all that.

Don't teach women alone in secret.

When you do teach, don't laugh loose and easy.

When you go to town for alms,

take another monk along.

If you got no monk to bring,

keep your mind on Buddha only.

These are your ground and your associations—

these two practices keep you safe,

Keep you able to teach with peace,

teach with joy, teach with freedom.

And beyond that: Don't hold to ideas

of high teachings or low teachings,

conditioned or unconditioned,

real or unreal—

Don't discriminate between

male and female in your mind.

Don't grasp at things as if they're solid,

don't act like you know when you don't,

Don't pretend to see what ain't visible.

That's the bodhisattva's ground.

All things are empty,

have no substance you can hold.

Nothing dwells forever,

nothing arises or ceases in truth.

This is what the wise ones know,

where the wise ones rest their minds.

Our confused discriminating thinking

divides things into real and unreal,

existent and non-existent,

born and unborn.

But you—you dwell in quiet places,

gathering your mind together,

stable and unmoving

like a mountain that won't shake.

View all phenomena

as having no substance,

like space itself,

without anything solid,

Not born, not emerging,

not moving, not retreating,

always abiding in one truth—

that's where you rest your mind.

If there's a monk

after I pass on

who enters this ground,

knows these associations,

When they teach this sutra

they won't have no fear.

Sometimes they'll go to a quiet room

and meditate properly,

Contemplate the meaning,

see the Dharma as it is.

Then they'll come out of meditation

to teach kings and princes,

Teach government officials,

teach priests and common people,

Opening up and sharing

this precious sutra,

Their heart at peace,

without any fear.

Mañjuśrī, this is the bodhisattva's

first peaceful practice—

Rest in this foundation,

and you can teach the Lotus

in the evil age to come

without losing yourself.

THIRD PRACTICE: HOW TO SPEAK AND WHAT TO HOLD IN YOUR HEART

"Now, Mañjuśrī, listen: After I'm gone, in the last age when the Dharma's dying out, if you want to teach this Lotus Sutra, you got to dwell in peaceful conduct.

Whether you're speaking it out loud or reading it quiet, don't talk bad about people and their failings. Don't speak about what's wrong with other sutras.

Don't look down on other teachers of the Dharma. Don't gossip about people's good points and bad points, their strengths and their shortcomings.

When it comes to those śrāvakas—those seeking their own enlightenment—don't call them out by name to shame them. Don't name them to praise them either. Don't breed resentment in your heart.

If you cultivate this peaceful mind, if you practice this kind of speaking, then people who come to hear you won't turn against you. And when they ask you hard questions, don't answer with small teachings—don't give them the little vehicle. Answer with the great vehicle, with the big vision that leads to complete wisdom, to knowing all that can be known."

THE BUDDHA SPEAKS IT IN RHYTHM AGAIN

Then the World-Honored One, wanting to make this meaning clear, spoke in rhythm:

The bodhisattva always loves

to teach in peace and safety.

They prepare a clean place,

set up a proper seat,

Anoint their body with oil,

wash off the dust and dirt,

put on fresh clean clothes—

clean outside and inside both.

Then they sit on the Dharma seat

and answer what they're asked.

If monks or nuns come,

if laymen or laywomen arrive,

If kings and princes show up,

if officials and common people gather,

They teach with subtle meaning,

with a gentle face and kind words.

If someone asks a hard question,

they answer according to the meaning.

They use causes and conditions,

they use parables and stories,

They explain and distinguish,

break it down so folks can understand.

By these skillful means

everyone's inspired,

Gradually they increase,

gradually they enter the Buddha Way.

Cast off your lazy mind,

throw away your slacking spirit,

Let go of your worries,

teach with a compassionate heart.

Day and night keep speaking

this supreme teaching,

Using countless causes,

innumerable parables,

Opening it up for all beings

so everyone rejoices.

Clothes and bedding,

food and medicine—

don't hope for any of that.

Just single-mindedly think:

"I teach for this reason only:

so I can achieve Buddhahood,

so all beings can achieve it too."

That's your great benefit,

That's your peaceful offering.

After I pass on,

if there's a monk who can teach

this wondrous Lotus Sutra,

Their heart won't hold jealousy,

hatred, or afflictions.

They won't have worries,

won't be cursed or insulted.

They won't fear

swords or sticks or violence.

They won't be driven out,

because they rest in patience.

The wise one practices like this,

cultivates their heart properly,

Dwells in peace and safety

just like I described.

That person's merit

over thousands of millions of ages—

you can't calculate it,

can't describe it with parables.

FOURTH PRACTICE: GREAT COMPASSION FOR THOSE WHO REJECT YOU

"Now, Mañjuśrī, there's one more practice—the fourth peaceful practice—for bodhisattvas teaching in the last age when the Dharma's dying out.

If you're teaching this Lotus Sutra, you got to keep your heart free from jealousy, from deceit, from lying. Don't look down on people learning the Buddha Way. Don't go searching for their faults.

Whether they're monks or nuns, laypeople, whether they're seeking the śrāvaka vehicle or the pratyekabuddha vehicle or the bodhisattva way—don't trouble them. Don't make them doubt themselves. Don't make them regret their path.

Don't say to them: 'You're far from the Way. You ain't never gonna reach complete knowledge. Why? Because you're lazy, that's why. Because you slack off on the path.'

And don't get into debates and arguments about the Dharma, don't get competitive about teaching.

Instead, toward all beings, generate great compassion. Toward all the Tathāgatas—all the Buddhas—hold them as loving fathers in your mind. Toward all the bodhisattvas, see them as your great teachers.

Toward the great bodhisattvas in all ten directions, always maintain deep respect in your heart. Bow down to them with reverence.

When you teach, teach all beings equally. Don't teach more to those who love the Dharma more. Don't measure out your teaching according to people's enthusiasm. Teach according to the Dharma itself—not too much, not too little, just what's right.

Even toward those who deeply love the teaching, don't give them extra just because they're eager."

THE FOURTH PRACTICE BRINGS PROTECTION AND COMMUNITY

"Mañjuśrī, if a bodhisattva in the last age—when the Dharma's dying—accomplishes this third peaceful practice, then when they teach this sutra, nobody can trouble them.

They'll find good companions to read and recite this sutra with them. They'll find large crowds coming to listen. And when people hear it, they'll be able to hold it. When they hold it, they'll be able to recite it. When they recite it, they'll be able to explain it. When they explain it, they'll be able to write it down, or get others to write it.

They'll make offerings to the sutra scroll, treat it with respect and honor, praise it and treasure it."

THE BUDDHA MAKES IT RHYTHM ONE MORE TIME

Then the World-Honored One, wanting to make this meaning clear, spoke in rhythm:

If you want to teach this sutra,

cast off jealousy and hatred and pride,

Cast off deceit and lying,

always practice straight honest conduct.

Don't look down on people,

don't argue about the Dharma,

Don't make others doubt and regret,

don't tell them they can't reach Buddhahood.

When this child of Buddha teaches,

they're always gentle, patient,

Compassionate to everyone,

never slacking off.

The great bodhisattvas of ten directions,

they practice the Way out of compassion—

Generate respect for them,

say "These are my great teachers."

Toward all the Buddha World-Honored Ones,

hold them as supreme fathers.

Break down your prideful heart

and you'll teach without obstruction.

This third practice is like this—

the wise should guard it well.

Single-heartedly dwell in peaceful practice

and countless people will respect you.

FOURTH PRACTICE: THE DEEPEST COMPASSION

"Now, Mañjuśrī, there's more to say about bodhisattvas in the last age when the Dharma's dying, those who hold this Lotus Sutra.

Toward those who've left home and those still in the household life, generate great compassion. Toward those who aren't bodhisattvas, generate even greater compassion—a vast compassion.

You should think like this: 'These people, they suffering a great loss. The Tathāgata teaches with skillful means according to what people need, but these folks—they don't hear it, don't know it, don't perceive it. They don't ask about it, don't believe it, don't understand it.

But even though they don't ask, don't believe, don't understand this sutra, when I reach complete perfect enlightenment, wherever they are, I'm gonna use my spiritual power and my wisdom power to draw them in, lead them here, make them dwell in this teaching.'

Mañjuśrī, if a bodhisattva after the Tathāgata passes on accomplishes this fourth practice, when they teach this sutra they won't make no mistakes, won't fall into error.

They'll always be honored by monks and nuns, by laymen and laywomen, by kings and princes, by government officials and common people, by priests and householders. They'll receive offerings, respect, honor, and praise.

The gods in the sky, wanting to hear the teaching, will constantly attend them. Whether they're in a village or a city or in an empty forest, if someone comes wanting to challenge them with hard questions, the gods day and night will protect them for the sake of the Dharma. They'll make everyone who hears rejoice.

Why? Because this sutra is protected by the spiritual power of all Buddhas—past, future, and present.

Mañjuśrī, this Lotus Sutra—in countless lands you can't even hear its name, much less see it, hold it, read it, or recite it!"

THE PARABLE OF THE KING'S CROWN JEWEL

"Mañjuśrī, let me tell you a story so you understand what I'm saying:

Imagine a powerful wheel-turning king—a king who rules with righteousness. He wants to bring all the nations under his rule using his authority. But some small kings won't submit to his command.

So this wheel-turning king raises up an army and goes to war against them. When he sees his soldiers fighting with courage, accomplishing difficult things, he's filled with joy. According to what they've accomplished, he gives rewards.

He gives them fields and houses, villages and cities. He gives them fine clothes and beautiful ornaments. He gives them all kinds of treasures—gold and silver, lapis lazuli, mother-of-pearl, agate, coral and amber. He gives them elephants and horses and carriages, slaves and servants, common people to work their lands.

But there's one thing he doesn't give: the bright jewel in his crown—the pearl on top of his head. Why? Because only the king has this one jewel, this unique treasure at the very crown of his head. If he gave it away, all his retinue would be shocked and amazed.

Well, Mañjuśrī, the Tathāgata is like this. Through the power of meditation and wisdom, he gained the territory of the Dharma. He rules over the three realms. But the demon kings wouldn't submit.

So the Tathāgata's worthy sages—his spiritual warriors—fought against those demons. When they showed great accomplishment, the Tathāgata's heart was filled with joy.

In the assembly of the four groups, he taught all kinds of sutras to make their hearts rejoice. He gave them the treasures of meditation and liberation, of undefiled roots and powers, of various dharmas. He also gave them the city of nirvāṇa, telling them they'd reached extinction. He guided their hearts and made them all rejoice.

But he didn't teach them this Lotus Sutra. Not yet.

Mañjuśrī, it's like when that wheel-turning king saw soldiers accomplish great things—things hard to believe—he was so joyful that he finally gave them that jewel that's hard to believe in, that pearl he kept so long in his crown, that treasure he never casually gave to anyone. Now he gives it.

The Tathāgata is like this. In the three realms he's the great Dharma king. With teachings he educates all beings. He sees his army of sages fighting against the five-aggregate demon, the affliction demon, and the death demon. He sees them accomplish great merit—destroying the three poisons, escaping the three realms, breaking through the demon's net.

At that time the Tathāgata is greatly joyful.

This Lotus Sutra can lead beings to complete wisdom. All the world finds it hard to believe—full of hatred for it, hard to trust. This is what he never taught before. But now he teaches it.

Mañjuśrī, this Lotus Sutra is the first teaching among all the Tathāgatas' teachings. Among all teachings, it's the most profound. At the very end he bestows it, just like that powerful king finally giving up that bright pearl he protected so long.

Mañjuśrī, this Lotus Sutra is the secret treasury of all Buddhas and Tathāgatas. Among all sutras, it stands at the very top. Through the long night I've guarded it, never carelessly teaching it. But now today I give it to you all and expound it fully."

THE BUDDHA BRINGS IT HOME IN RHYTHM

Then the World-Honored One, wanting to make this meaning sink deep, spoke in rhythm:

Always practice patience,

have compassion for everyone—

Only then can you teach

this sutra the Buddha praises.

In the last age,

those who hold this teaching

Toward those in homes and those who left them,

toward those who aren't bodhisattvas,

Should generate compassion.

If they don't hear this,

Don't believe this sutra,

they're suffering a great loss.

When I achieve Buddhahood

I'll use skillful means

To teach them this dharma,

make them dwell in it.

It's like that powerful

wheel-turning king:

When soldiers fight with courage

he rewards them with everything—

Elephants, horses, carriages,

beautiful ornaments,

Fields and houses,

villages and cities,

Fine clothes

and all kinds of treasures,

Slaves and servants and wealth—

joyfully he gives it all.

But when someone shows real courage,

does something truly difficult,

The king takes off that crown jewel,

that bright pearl, and gives it to them.

The Tathāgata is like this:

He's king of all the dharmas,

With the great power of patience,

with the treasure-house of wisdom,

With great compassion

he teaches the world according to truth.

Seeing all people

receiving suffering and trouble,

Wanting to seek liberation,

fighting against demons,

For these beings

he teaches all kinds of dharmas,

With great skillful means

he teaches various sutras.

Once he knows beings

have gained their power,

At the very end

he teaches this Lotus,

Like that king taking off his crown,

giving that bright pearl.

This sutra is most honored,

highest among all sutras.

I've always guarded it,

never carelessly revealed it.

Now is the right time

to teach it to you all.

After I pass on,

those seeking the Buddha Way,

Wanting to teach this sutra

in peace and safety,

Should draw near

to these four practices.

Those who read this sutra

will always be free from worry,

Also free from sickness,

their face bright and fresh.

They won't be born into poverty,

lowliness, or ugliness.

All beings love to see them,

admire them like sages.

The children of heaven

will serve and attend them.

Swords won't touch them,

poison can't harm them.

If someone tries to curse them,

that person's mouth will close up.

They'll walk without fear

like a lion king.

Their wisdom light will shine

bright as the sun itself.

And in their dreams

they'll see only wonderful things.

THE BEAUTIFUL DREAMS OF THE DHARMA HOLDER

They'll see the Tathāgatas

sitting on lion thrones,

Surrounded by assemblies of monks,

teaching the Dharma.

They'll see dragons and spirits,

asuras and others,

Numerous as Ganges sands,

palms joined in respect.

They'll see themselves

teaching the Dharma to them all.

They'll see the Buddhas

with bodies colored like gold,

Radiating boundless light,

illuminating everything,

With brahma voices

expounding all the dharmas.

The Buddha teaching the four groups

the supreme dharma—

They'll see themselves standing there,

palms joined, praising Buddha,

Hearing the Dharma with joy,

making offerings,

Receiving dhāraṇī,

attaining wisdom that won't retreat.

The Buddha knows their mind

has entered deep into the Buddha Way,

So he gives them a prophecy,

tells them they'll achieve perfect enlightenment:

"You, good person,

in the age to come

Will gain the boundless wisdom,

the great Way of Buddha.

Your land will be pure,

vast and beyond compare.

You too will have four groups

with palms joined, hearing the Dharma."

They'll also see themselves

in mountain forests,

Practicing good dharmas,

realizing true reality,

Entering deep into meditation,

seeing Buddhas in ten directions—

Those Buddhas with golden bodies,

adorned with a hundred blessed marks.

Hearing the Dharma, teaching it to others—

always having these beautiful dreams.

They'll also dream they're a king

who renounces palace and family,

Gives up the supreme five desires,

goes to the place of enlightenment,

Sits under the bodhi tree

on a lion throne,

Seeks the Way for seven days,

obtains the wisdom of all Buddhas.

Having achieved the supreme Way,

they rise and turn the Dharma wheel,

Teaching the four groups

for thousands of millions of ages,

Teaching the undefiled wondrous Dharma,

liberating countless beings.

Then finally entering nirvāṇa

like smoke disappearing, like a lamp going out.

If in the evil age

you teach this first dharma,

That person gains great benefit,

all these merits I've described.

END OF CHAPTER FOURTEEN: HOW TO KEEP YOUR SOUL CLEAN WHEN THE WORLD'S GONE WRONG

---

This chapter is survival wisdom for spiritual teachers in corrupt times. The Buddha ain't talking theory here—he's giving practical instructions for how to keep your soul clean when the world's gone wrong.

The four practices are like concentric circles of protection:

1. Your inner ground (patience, non-grasping, seeing clearly)

2. Your outer associations (who you don't run with, what you don't do)

3. Your speech and heart (how you talk, what you hold inside)

4. Your ultimate compassion (even for those who reject you)

The crown jewel parable is central: The Lotus Sutra is what the Buddha held back until his spiritual warriors had proven themselves, until they'd fought through their own demons and poisons. It's the final gift, the supreme teaching, given only when folks are ready.

And the beautiful dreams at the end—that's confirmation. That's how you know you're on the right path. When you hold this teaching right, it holds you back. You walk protected. Your dreams become prophecies. Even sleep becomes teaching.

This is practical mysticism. This is how you teach the highest truth in the lowest times without becoming what you're fighting against.

CHAPTER FIFTEEN: WHEN THE UNDERGROUND ARMY ROSE UP

(從地湧出品 - Springing Up from the Earth)

---

THE OFFER THAT GOT TURNED DOWN

Now at that time, all them bodhisattvas from other worlds—countless worlds beyond counting, more than eight Ganges rivers full of sand—they stood up in that great gathering. They pressed their palms together, bowed down low, and said to the Buddha:

"World-Honored One, if you let us—when you gone from this world, after you passed on—we will stay right here in this Śākyamuni realm, this troubled world called Sahā. We will work hard, we will stay diligent, we will protect this sutra, read it, recite it, write it down, make offerings to it. We will teach it far and wide in this land here."

But then the Buddha, he told all them bodhisattvas—all them great beings from other worlds:

"Stop. Stop right there, good sons. You don't need to protect this sutra. You don't need to worry yourselves about it.

Why's that?

Because my Śākyamuni world—this world right here—it already got 60,000 times the sands of the Ganges River worth of great bodhisattvas. And every single one of them bodhisattvas, they each got their own retinue—60,000 Ganges-sands worth of followers each.

And these people—all these beings right here—they able to protect this sutra after I'm gone. They can hold it, read it, recite it, teach it wide."

THE EARTH SPLITS WIDE OPEN

Now when the Buddha spoke them words, the whole Śākyamuni world—all three thousand great thousand realms of it—the ground started shaking and trembling and splitting apart.

And out from them cracks, out from underneath, there came bursting forth countless thousands, tens of thousands, millions, billions of bodhisattvas—great bodhisattvas, mighty bodhisattvas—all at the same time, all rising up together like a flood, like a army, like the morning breaking.

And these bodhisattvas, every one of them had bodies of gold—golden like the sunrise, golden like pure truth. They all had the thirty-two marks, they all shining with boundless light, they all radiant beyond measure.

And all along, all this time before now, they'd been living right underneath this Śākyamuni world—down below in the empty space beneath this realm—just dwelling there, waiting there, ready there.

These bodhisattvas, they heard Śākyamuni Buddha's voice when he spoke, and they came up from below. They answered the call.

THE VAST NUMBERS THAT NOBODY CAN COUNT

Now every one of these bodhisattvas, they was leaders—great leaders, mighty leaders—leading the masses, guiding the multitudes.

And each one was bringing their own followers with them:

Some had retinues of 60,000 Ganges-sands worth,

Some had 50,000 Ganges-sands,

Some 40,000, some 30,000, some 20,000, some 10,000 Ganges-sands of followers.

And then there was those with one Ganges-sand worth,

Or half a Ganges-sand,

Or a quarter, or one-eighth,

Or down to a thousand million billion nayutas fraction of a Ganges-sand.

And some had a thousand million billion nayutas of disciples,

Some had a hundred million or ten million,

Some had a million, hundred thousand, down to ten thousand.

Some had a thousand, a hundred, down to ten.

Some brought five disciples, some four, some three, some two, some just one.

And some came alone—single and solitary, loving the quiet path, walking the remote road by themselves.

On and on like this—countless, boundless, beyond all calculation, beyond all comparison, beyond all arithmetic, beyond what numbers can measure or words can tell.

THE GREAT ASSEMBLY AND THE LONG PRAISE

When all these bodhisattvas came up from the earth, they each went to the jeweled stupa in the sky—to where Abundant Treasures Tathāgata and Śākyamuni Buddha was sitting together.

When they got there, they bowed down before both World-Honored Ones, touched their heads to the feet of both Buddhas. They went to all the Buddha-copies sitting under the jeweled trees on lion thrones throughout the realms, and they bowed before each one too.

They circled them three times to the right, then stood with palms pressed together in deep respect. And they praised the Buddhas with all manner of bodhisattva praise-forms—praising and honoring, then standing to one side, gazing up joyfully at the two World-Honored Ones.

Now these great bodhisattvas, from the time they first burst forth from the earth, they was praising the Buddhas with all kinds of bodhisattva praise-forms. And this praising—this honoring and celebrating—it went on for FIFTY SMALL KALPAS. Fifty ages. Fifty long stretches of time.

And during all this time, Śākyamuni Buddha sat there silent. And all the four assemblies—they was silent too. Fifty small kalpas they was silent.

But because of the Buddha's spiritual power, he made it seem to that great assembly like only half a day had passed. Half a day! Time got bent, time got folded, time got compressed by the Buddha's power.

And also because of the Buddha's spiritual power, the four assemblies could see all them bodhisattvas filling up the empty space of countless hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of realms—filling it all up, everywhere you looked.

THE FOUR GREAT LEADERS STEP FORWARD

Now among all that vast bodhisattva multitude, there was four leaders—four chief guides, four head teachers:

The first was named SUPERIOR PRACTICE (上行 - Viśiṣṭhacāritra),

The second was named BOUNDLESS PRACTICE (無邊行 - Anantacāritra),

The third was named PURE PRACTICE (淨行 - Viśuddhacāritra),

The fourth was named WELL-ESTABLISHED PRACTICE (安立行 - Supratiṣṭhitacāritra).

These four bodhisattvas—they was the highest leaders, the chief guides among all that crowd.

They came forward in front of the great assembly. They pressed their palms together, gazed at Śākyamuni Buddha, and asked respectfully:

"World-Honored One, are you well? Do you have few sicknesses? Few troubles? Are you dwelling in peace and ease? Are the beings you teaching—are they easy to teach? Are they receiving instruction without resistance? We hope you not worn out, not exhausted, not burdened down by the work."

THE FOUR LEADERS SPEAK IN VERSE

Then at that time, them four great bodhisattvas spoke in verse, saying:

Is the World-Honored One dwelling in peace,

with few sicknesses, few troubles?

Teaching all these living beings—

do you find yourself growing weary?

And are these living beings

easy to teach, easy to reach?

We hope they don't make the World-Honored One

tired, worn down, heavy-burdened.

THE BUDDHA SAYS: "IT AIN'T BEEN HARD"

Then the World-Honored One, there in the midst of that vast bodhisattva assembly, he said:

"Yes, yes, good sons. The Tathāgata is dwelling in peace. I got few sicknesses, few troubles. And these living beings—they easy to teach. There ain't been no weariness, no fatigue.

Why's that?

Because these living beings, life after life, world after world, age after age, they been receiving my teaching all along. And also in ages past, under previous Buddhas, they showed respect and reverence, and they planted all kinds of good roots.

So when these beings first saw my body, when they first heard what I was saying, they believed right away. They received it and entered into the Tathāgata's wisdom—just like that.

Now there's some exceptions—those who studied the small vehicle before, the lesser teachings. But even those people, I'm making sure they hear this sutra now, so they can enter into the Buddha's wisdom too."

THE GREAT BODHISATTVAS REJOICE

Then at that time, all them great bodhisattvas spoke in verse, saying:

Excellent! Excellent!

Great Hero, World-Honored One,

All these living beings—

they easy to teach, easy to guide.

They able to ask the Buddhas

about deep wisdom, profound truth.

Hearing it, they believe and practice—

and we rejoice along with them!

THE BUDDHA PRAISES HIS STUDENTS

Then the World-Honored One praised them chief bodhisattvas, saying:

"Excellent! Excellent, good sons! You able to rejoice along with the Tathāgata—able to be glad in the Tathāgata's work. That's good, that's real good."

MAITREYA'S MIND STARTS SPINNING

Now at that time, Maitreya Bodhisattva and 8,000 Ganges-sands worth of other bodhisattvas—they all had this thought arise in their minds:

"All this time up till now—from way back, from the beginning—we never seen, never heard tell of such a vast assembly of great bodhisattvas bursting up from the earth like this, standing before the World-Honored One, palms pressed together, making offerings, asking after the Tathāgata.

Where did they come from? What is this?"

MAITREYA ASKS THE EXPLOSIVE QUESTION

So Maitreya Bodhisattva, knowing what was on the minds of them 8,000 Ganges-sands worth of bodhisattvas—and wanting to clear up his own doubts too—he pressed his palms together, faced the Buddha, and asked in verse:

Countless thousands of millions of billions—

this vast assembly of bodhisattvas,

we never seen before, never once.

Will the Honored One of Two Feet explain:

Where did they come from?

What causes and conditions brought them here?

They got giant bodies, great spiritual powers,

wisdom that can't be fathomed—

Their will and intention is rock-solid firm,

they got the power of great patience and endurance,

living beings love to see them—

but where they come from?

Every single one of these bodhisattvas

got their own retinue of followers,

and the numbers got no measure—

like the sands of the Ganges River.

Some of these great bodhisattvas

leading 60,000 Ganges-sands worth,

and all these vast crowds

seeking the Buddha-way with one mind.

These great teachers—

all 60,000 Ganges-sands of them—

they come together to make offerings to Buddha

and to protect and uphold this sutra.

Some leading 50,000 Ganges-sands,

and the numbers go even higher than that.

Some got 40,000, some 30,000,

some 20,000 down to 10,000,

Some with a thousand, a hundred,

down to one Ganges-sand worth,

or half, or a third or quarter,

or down to one billion-millionth fraction,

Thousands of millions of nayutas,

ten thousands of billions of disciples,

down to half a billion,

and the numbers keep going higher still.

A hundred thousand down to ten thousand,

a thousand down to a hundred,

fifty, ten,

down to three, two, one,

Or coming alone with no retinue,

loving the solitary path—

all coming to the Buddha's place,

and the numbers beyond even that.

If someone tried to count this vast assembly,

using counting-sticks for more than Ganges-sand kalpas,

they still wouldn't be able to finish knowing—

couldn't reach the end.

These great beings of awesome virtue,

these vigorous bodhisattvas—

who taught them the Dharma?

Who educated and perfected them?

Under whom did they first set their minds on enlightenment?

What Buddha's teaching did they praise?

Whose sutras did they receive and uphold?

What Buddha's way did they practice and study?

These bodhisattvas

with spiritual powers and great wisdom-strength—

the earth in the four directions split open,

and they all burst forth from the middle.

World-Honored One, since ancient times till now

I never seen such a thing.

Please tell us where they from,

what country, what land they came from.

I been traveling through all the nations,

but I never seen this assembly before.

In this whole crowd here

I don't recognize a single person—

Suddenly they bursting from the earth.

Please explain the causes and conditions.

Right now in this great assembly—

countless hundreds of thousands of billions—

All these bodhisattvas

want to know about this matter.

The root and branch of these bodhisattvas,

the beginning and end of their causes and conditions—

World-Honored One of boundless virtue,

we only asking you: please resolve our doubts.

THE BUDDHAS IN ALL DIRECTIONS WONDER TOO

Then at that time, all them emanation-bodies of Śākyamuni Buddha—all them Buddha-copies who came from countless thousands of millions of billions of other lands, sitting on lion thrones under jeweled trees throughout the eight directions—each one of their attendants saw this great assembly of bodhisattvas bursting up from the earth in the four directions of the three thousand great thousand worlds, rising up into empty space.

And each attendant said to their Buddha:

"World-Honored One, where did all these countless, boundless, innumerable bodhisattvas come from?"

And each of them Buddhas told their attendant:

"Good sons, wait just a moment. There's a bodhisattva here named Maitreya, who Śākyamuni Buddha done given a prophecy to—said he gonna be the next Buddha after this one. He already asked about this very matter. The Buddha about to answer him now. You'll hear it yourselves when he explains."

THE BUDDHA TELLS MAITREYA TO GET READY

Then at that time, Śākyamuni Buddha told Maitreya Bodhisattva:

"Excellent! Excellent, Ajita! That you able to ask the Buddha about such a great matter—this is good, this is right.

Now all of you need to gather yourselves together with one mind. Put on the armor of vigor and diligence. Make your intention firm and solid.

Because the Tathāgata is about to reveal and display and proclaim:

The wisdom of all Buddhas,

The Buddhas' self-mastered spiritual power,

The Buddhas' lion-like charging forth,

The Buddhas' awesome and mighty great force."

THE BUDDHA SPEAKS IN VERSE: PAY ATTENTION NOW

Then the World-Honored One, wanting to say this teaching again, spoke in verse:

You need to be vigorous with one mind—

I'm about to explain this matter.

Don't let yourself have doubts or regrets.

The Buddha's wisdom can't be thought or measured.

Now bring forth your power of faith,

dwell in patience and goodness.

This Dharma you never heard before—

today you all gonna hear it.

I'm comforting you now,

don't let yourself harbor doubts or fears.

The Buddha don't speak no falsehoods,

his wisdom can't be calculated.

The supreme Dharma he obtained—

it's deep, can't be discriminated or divided up.

And now I'm about to speak it,

so all of you listen with one mind.

THE SHOCKING TRUTH BEGINS TO EMERGE

Then after the World-Honored One finished speaking this verse, he told Maitreya Bodhisattva:

"I'm now declaring to all of you in this great assembly—

Ajita! These great bodhisattvas, these innumerable, uncountable, inconceivable numbers of great beings bursting up from the earth that you never seen before—

After I attained supreme perfect enlightenment here in this Śākyamuni world, I taught and guided these bodhisattvas. I tamed their minds, caused them to give rise to the intention for the Way.

All these bodhisattvas have been dwelling right here—underneath this Śākyamuni world, in the empty space below this realm.

As for the sutras and scriptures, they read them, recite them, understand them thoroughly. They think about them, analyze them, contemplate them correctly and remember them right.

Ajita, these good sons—they don't love being in crowds with a lot of talking. They always love quiet places where they can practice vigorously without ever resting.

They don't depend on human or heavenly realms for their dwelling.

They always love deep wisdom without obstacles or hindrances. And they always love the Dharma of all Buddhas. With single mind and vigorous effort, they seeking supreme wisdom without cease."

THE BUDDHA SPEAKS IN VERSE: THESE ARE MY CHILDREN

Then the World-Honored One, wanting to state this meaning again, spoke in verse:

Ajita, you need to know:

These great bodhisattvas,

for countless kalpas past,

been practicing Buddha-wisdom.

Every one of them is who I taught,

who I caused to give rise to the great Way-seeking mind.

These are my children,

dwelling and depending on this world.

Always practicing ascetic practices,

志樂 loving quiet places,

leaving behind the noise and confusion of crowds,

not loving a lot of talk.

Children like these,

they studying and practicing my Way and Dharma.

Day and night always vigorous,

for the sake of seeking the Buddha-way.

They dwelling underneath the Śākyamuni world,

down in the empty space below,

their will and mindfulness power rock-solid firm,

always diligently seeking wisdom,

Speaking all manner of wonderful Dharma,

their hearts without any fear.

I sat underneath the Bodhi tree

in the city of Gayā,

Attained the supreme perfect enlightenment,

turned the unsurpassed Dharma wheel.

And then I taught and guided them,

caused them to first give rise to the Way-seeking mind.

Now they all dwelling in non-retreat,

and they all gonna attain Buddhahood.

I'm speaking the real truth now—

all of you need to believe with one mind:

From the distant past till now

I been teaching and guiding this assembly.

MAITREYA'S MIND CAN'T TAKE IT: THE IMPOSSIBLE MATH

Now at that time, Maitreya Bodhisattva and countless other bodhisattvas—their minds filled up with doubt. They bewildered, amazed, never experienced nothing like this before.

And they thought:

"How could the World-Honored One in such a short time teach and guide such countless, boundless, innumerable great bodhisattvas and cause them to dwell in supreme perfect enlightenment?"

So Maitreya said directly to the Buddha:

"World-Honored One, when the Tathāgata was still a prince, he left the palace of the Śākya clan. He went not far from the city of Gayā, sat at the place of enlightenment, and attained supreme perfect enlightenment.

From that time till now, it's only been a little more than forty years.

World-Honored One, how could you in this short time accomplish such a great Buddha-work? How could you, with the Buddha's power and the Buddha's merit, teach and guide such countless great bodhisattvas to the point where they gonna attain supreme perfect enlightenment?

THE STORY NOBODY CAN BELIEVE

"World-Honored One, this great assembly of bodhisattvas—even if someone spent a thousand million billion kalpas trying to count them, they couldn't finish, couldn't reach the end.

These beings—from the distant, remote past—they been planting good roots under countless, boundless Buddhas. They done perfected the bodhisattva way. They always been practicing the pure conduct.

World-Honored One, such a thing as this—the world finds it hard to believe.

It's like if there was a man with beautiful features and black hair, only twenty-five years old, and he pointed at a hundred-year-old man and said, 'This is my son.' And the hundred-year-old man also pointed at the young one and said, 'This is my father who gave birth to me and raised me.'

Now that would be hard to believe, wouldn't it?"

THE DEPTH OF THE PROBLEM

"It's the same with the Buddha. You only attained the Way a short while ago—that's the truth of it.

But this great assembly of bodhisattvas—they already been practicing for countless thousands of millions of billions of kalpas for the sake of the Buddha-way. They been practicing vigorously, diligently.

They skilled at entering, leaving, and dwelling in countless hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of samādhis—deep meditations beyond measure. They obtained great spiritual powers. For a long time they been practicing pure conduct.

They able to practice good dharmas in proper sequence, step by step. They skillful at questions and answers. They treasures among human beings. They extremely rare in all the worlds.

And yet today the World-Honored One is saying that when you first attained the Buddha-way, you caused them to first give rise to the aspiration, then taught and guided them, directing them toward supreme perfect enlightenment.

World-Honored One, you ain't been Buddha for long—and yet you been able to accomplish this great work of merit!

Now we do believe in the Buddha. We believe whatever the Buddha says is appropriate to the situation. We know the Buddha's words never empty or false. We know the Buddha's knowledge penetrates everything thoroughly.

But still—

Those bodhisattvas who newly given rise to intention, who just starting out—if they hear these words after the Buddha passes away, they might not believe and accept it. And that might cause them to commit the sin of slandering the Dharma, breaking faith with the teaching.

So please, World-Honored One, explain this for us. Remove our doubts. And also for the good sons and daughters of future ages—when they hear about this matter, let them not have doubts either."

MAITREYA SPEAKS IN VERSE: THIS DON'T ADD UP

Then Maitreya Bodhisattva, wanting to state this meaning again, spoke in verse:

Long ago the Buddha left the Śākya clan,

went not far from the city of Gayā,

sat underneath the Bodhi tree—

and since then it ain't been that long.

But all these children of the Buddha,

their numbers beyond all counting—

they already been walking the Buddha-way for ages,

dwelling in spiritual powers,

Well-learned in the bodhisattva path,

unstained by worldly dharmas,

like lotus flowers in the water

bursting up from the earth.

All of them arising with respectful hearts,

standing before the World-Honored One.

This matter is hard to think about—

how can it be believed?

The Buddha attained the Way so recently,

yet what he accomplished is so vast!

Please remove the doubts of this assembly,

explain it truly and clearly.

It's like a young man in his prime,

only twenty-five years old,

pointing to people a hundred years old,

their hair white and faces wrinkled,

Saying, 'These are the ones I gave birth to.'

And the sons also saying, 'He is our father.'

The father young and the sons old—

the whole world wouldn't believe it.

The World-Honored One is like that too:

You attained the Way so recently.

But these bodhisattvas,

their will is firm without weakness or cowardice.

For countless kalpas past

they been practicing the bodhisattva way.

They skillful at difficult questions and answers,

their minds without any fear.

Their patience-hearts decided and determined,

dignified and proper with great virtue.

Praised by Buddhas in the ten directions,

able to explain distinctions skillfully.

They don't love being in crowds of people,

they always prefer deep meditation.

For the sake of seeking the Buddha-way,

they dwelling in the empty space below.

We done heard all this from the Buddha—

about this matter we got no doubt.

But we asking Buddha for the sake of future generations:

Explain it so they can understand.

If someone has doubts about this sutra,

doesn't believe it,

they gonna fall into the evil paths right away.

So please explain it now:

How did you, in such a short time,

teach these countless bodhisattvas,

cause them to give rise to the aspiration,

and establish them in the ground of non-retreating?

END OF CHAPTER FIFTEEN: WHEN THE UNDERGROUND ARMY ROSE UP

---

This is the chapter where everything the assembly thought they knew gets turned upside down. They thought Śākyamuni Buddha was a man who lived 80 years ago, who attained enlightenment 40 years back under a tree. But now he's revealing students—vast numbers of students, accomplished students, ancient students—who've been practicing for countless ages.

The math don't work. The timeline don't work. It's impossible.

Unless... unless the Buddha is something other than what they thought. Unless his life is deeper than one lifetime. Unless time itself works different when it comes to the Dharma-body of a Buddha.

That's what Chapter 16 gonna reveal: the Buddha's eternal life, his ancient original enlightenment. But here in Chapter 15, we sitting in the shock of it—in Maitreya's bewildered question, in the image of the 25-year-old father with hundred-year-old sons.

The four great leaders got names that tell you what practice looks like when it's been going on for ages:

- Superior Practice: Not just practicing, but practicing at the highest level

- Boundless Practice: Practice without edges, without limits

- Pure Practice: Practice that ain't corrupted, ain't compromised

- Well-Established Practice: Practice that's rooted deep, that can't be shaken

They burst from the earth—from underneath, from below, from the hidden places. They been there all along, preparing, waiting, practicing in the dark, in the quiet, in the deep.

That's the power of this chapter: the revelation that the real work been happening out of sight, beneath the surface, in the underground. And when the time comes, when the call goes out, that hidden strength rises up—a vast army, a golden flood, a force that fills all space.

The church been praying in the basement all this time. And now it's time to come upstairs.

CHAPTER SIXTEEN: HOW LONG THE TATHĀGATA'S BEEN ALIVE

A Blues Translation from Kumārajīva's Classical Chinese

---

THE TRIPLE REQUEST—BELIEVE WHAT I'M ABOUT TO TELL YOU

At that time, the Buddha spoke to all them bodhisattvas and the whole great assembly. He said:

"Good sons, y'all need to believe and understand the truthful words of the Tathāgata—the honest-to-God truth I'm speaking."

And he told that great crowd again:

"Y'all need to believe and understand the truthful words of the Tathāgata."

And once more he told that assembly:

"Y'all need to believe and understand the truthful words of the Tathāgata."

Now at that time, all them bodhisattvas—that great multitude with Maitreya leading them—they pressed their palms together and said to the Buddha:

"World-Honored One, we asking you please—just tell us. We gonna believe what the Buddha says. We gonna receive it and hold it true."

They said this three times over. Three times they asked. And then they said again:

"Please, World-Honored One, just tell us. We gonna believe what the Buddha says. We gonna receive it."

---

THE SECRET THE BUDDHA'S BEEN KEEPING—LISTEN NOW

Then the World-Honored One, seeing them bodhisattvas ask three times and not stopping, he told them:

"Y'all listen close now. Listen to the secret spiritual power of the Tathāgata.

All the world—all the gods, all the humans, all the asuras—everybody thinks the same thing. They say: 'This Śākyamuni Buddha, he left the palace of the Shakya clan. He went not far from the city of Gaya. He sat down at the place of enlightenment and got that supreme perfect enlightenment—that anuttara-samyak-sambodhi.'

But good sons, listen to what I'm telling you:

I actually became a Buddha countless, boundless, hundred thousand million billion nayuta uncountable ages ago.

That's the truth."

---

THE CALCULATION YOU CAN'T EVEN CALCULATE

"Let me paint you a picture. Say you took five hundred thousand million billion nayuta asamkhya three-thousand-great-thousand worlds—every single one of them worlds. And say you ground them all down into dust, into powder, into the finest grains you ever saw.

Then say you started walking east. And after you passed five hundred thousand million billion nayuta asamkhya countries, you put down one single grain of dust. Just one speck.

And then you kept on walking east like that, passing through all them worlds, dropping one dust grain here, skipping some worlds, dropping one there, walking and walking till every last speck of dust was gone—all used up.

Now good sons, what you think? Could anybody—any mathematician, any calculator, any scholar with the best mind—could they sit down and figure out how many worlds you passed through? Could they count them up and know the number?"

Maitreya Bodhisattva and all the others answered the Buddha, saying:

"World-Honored One, them worlds—they countless, they boundless. They ain't something numbers can know. They ain't something the mind can reach. Even all them voice-hearers and pratyekabuddhas—with all their wisdom free from outflows—they couldn't think their way to knowing that limit, couldn't calculate that number.

We standing here on the ground of non-backsliding, and even we don't understand this matter.

World-Honored One, them worlds—they countless, they boundless."

Then the Buddha told that great bodhisattva assembly:

"Good sons, I'm gonna tell you clear and plain right now.

Take all them worlds—the ones where you put down dust and the ones where you didn't—grind them all down into dust. Every single grain of dust, that's one age, one kalpa.

The time since I became a Buddha is even MORE than that. We talking hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of nayutas of uncountable ages BEYOND all them dust specks.

And from that time till now, I been constantly here in this Saha world teaching the Dharma, guiding beings. And I also been in hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of nayutas of uncountable other countries, leading and benefiting living beings."

---

ABOUT THEM OTHER BUDDHAS I TOLD YOU ABOUT

"Good sons, during all that time—all them ages—I talked about Dipamkara Buddha and others. I said they entered into nirvāṇa, passed away into final rest.

But all that—all them stories—they was just skillful means, just ways of teaching that fit what folks could understand.

Good sons, when living beings come to me, I look at them with the Buddha-eye. I see their faith, see their roots—are they sharp or are they dull? Are they ready or not ready?

And according to what they need to hear—according to how they need to be taught—I show up in different places, call myself by different names, say I'm different ages—young or old, early or late in my teaching career.

Sometimes I even say: 'I'm about to enter nirvāṇa soon. I'm getting ready to pass on.'

And I use all kinds of skillful means, all kinds of subtle and wonderful teachings to make living beings feel joy, to help them generate happiness.

Good sons, the Tathāgata sees that some living beings—they love the small teachings. They got thin virtue and heavy dirt on them. For people like that, I tell them:

'When I was young, I left home and got that supreme perfect enlightenment.'

But the truth is, I been a Buddha for a vast, long time—so long you can't measure it. I just use skillful means to teach living beings, to help them enter the Buddha-path. That's why I say what I say."

---

EVERYTHING THE BUDDHA SAYS IS TRUE—EVEN WHEN IT LOOKS LIKE A LIE

"Good sons, all the sutras the Tathāgata speaks—every teaching, every scripture—they all for saving living beings, for setting them free.

Sometimes I talk about my own life.

Sometimes I talk about someone else's life.

Sometimes I show my own body.

Sometimes I show someone else's body.

Sometimes I talk about my own affairs.

Sometimes I talk about someone else's affairs.

But everything I say—every word spoken—is real. It ain't false. It ain't empty.

Why's that?

Because the Tathāgata sees the three worlds just like they really are. He sees them truly, sees them clearly.

There ain't no birth and death.

Ain't no retreating, ain't no going forth.

Ain't nobody really in the world, ain't nobody really passing out of it.

It ain't real, it ain't false.

It ain't like this, it ain't like that.

It ain't the way the three worlds see the three worlds.

All this—the Tathāgata sees it clearly, sees it plainly, without a single mistake, without one error.

Living beings got all kinds of natures, all kinds of desires, all kinds of actions, all kinds of thoughts and imaginings running through their minds.

I want them to plant good roots. So I use all kinds of causes and conditions, all kinds of parables and stories, all kinds of words and ways of speaking. I teach the Dharma in every way you can imagine.

The Buddha-work I do—it ain't never stopped. It ain't never rested. Not for one single moment.

And so—I been a Buddha for a vast, great, long time. My lifespan is countless uncountable ages. I'm constantly dwelling here. I don't pass away."

---

WHY THE BUDDHA PLAYS LIKE HE'S DYING WHEN HE AIN'T

"Good sons, the lifespan I got—the life I achieved when I first walked the bodhisattva path—that lifespan still ain't finished yet. It's still going. It's twice as long as all them ages I just told you about, and more.

But even though I ain't really gonna pass into extinction, I announce it anyway. I say: 'I'm about to enter final nirvāṇa.'

The Tathāgata uses this skillful means to teach living beings.

Why's that? Why do I do it this way?

Because if the Buddha stayed in the world a long time—if I was always here, always visible, always present—then people with thin virtue, people who ain't planted good roots yet, people who poor and low-down and greedy and attached to the five desires, people caught up in the net of wrong thoughts and false views—

If they saw the Tathāgata always here and never passing away, they'd get arrogant. They'd get lazy and complacent. They wouldn't feel that sense of 'This is rare! This is precious! This is hard to meet!'

They wouldn't have respect. They wouldn't have reverence.

So the Tathāgata uses skillful means and says to them:

'Monks, you should know—it's hard to meet a Buddha in the world. It's a rare thing. It don't happen often.'

Why's that? Why's it so rare?

Because people with thin virtue—they can go through countless hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of ages, and some might see a Buddha, but some won't. They might never see one at all.

Because of this—because it's so rare—I say to them:

'Monks, the Tathāgata is hard to see. It ain't easy to meet the Tathāgata.'

When living beings hear these words, they bound to feel it—that longing, that sense of how rare it is, how hard it is to meet a Buddha. Their hearts gonna fill up with yearning and longing. They gonna thirst after the Buddha.

And then they gonna plant good roots.

That's why the Tathāgata—even though he ain't really passing into extinction—he says he's gonna pass away.

He says he's dying when he ain't dying.

This ain't false. This is medicine."

---

THE PARABLE OF THE GOOD DOCTOR AND HIS POISONED CHILDREN

"And also, good sons, all the Buddhas and Tathāgatas—their way is just like this. Everything they do is real, ain't nothing false. It's all to save living beings.

Let me tell you a story:

Say there was a good doctor—a excellent physician, wise and intelligent, thoroughly understanding medicines and prescriptions, skilled at curing all kinds of sicknesses.

This man had a lot of sons—maybe ten, maybe twenty, could be a hundred of them.

One day he had to leave on some business. He traveled far away to another country.

After the father left, all his children back home—they found some poison and drank it by mistake. They didn't know what they was drinking.

That poison started working in their bodies. They started rolling on the ground, writhing and crying out, confused and tormented.

Then the father came back home.

Some of his sons—the ones who drunk the poison—some of them had completely lost their minds. But some still had a bit of sense left in them, still had some clarity.

When they saw their father coming from far off, they all felt great joy. They bowed down, they greeted him, they said:

'Welcome back safe and sound, father! We been foolish. We drunk poison by mistake. Please heal us! Please give us back our lives! Please don't let us die!'

The father saw his children suffering like that—in such pain, in such agony. So right away, following all the medical texts and prescriptions, he went and gathered excellent medicinal herbs—herbs with perfect color, perfect fragrance, perfect taste. All the qualities was just right.

He ground them up, sifted them, mixed them together real fine, and gave the medicine to his children to drink. And he said to them:

'This here is excellent medicine. The color is excellent, the fragrance is excellent, the taste is excellent. Y'all take this and drink it down. It's gonna remove your suffering real quick. You won't have no more pain, no more sickness.'"

---

SOME TAKE THE MEDICINE, SOME DON'T

"Now among all them children, the ones who ain't completely lost their minds—the ones who still had some sense—when they saw that good medicine, saw the color and smelled the fragrance, they took it right away and drank it down.

And their sickness was completely healed. All the poison left their bodies.

But the other children—the ones who had completely lost their minds—when they saw their father come home, they was also happy. They also asked him to heal them, asked for the medicine.

But when he gave them that medicine, they wouldn't take it. They wouldn't drink it.

Why not?

Because the poison had gone so deep. It had messed up their minds so bad, they had lost their original nature. Even though that medicine had perfect color and perfect fragrance, they looked at it and thought it was ugly. They thought it was nasty.

The father thought to himself:

'My poor children. They so pitiful. The poison done made them crazy, done made them insane. Even though they happy to see me, even though they begging me to heal them—when I give them this good medicine, they won't take it.

I got to set up some skillful means to make them take this medicine.'

So he said to them:

'Y'all need to know something. I'm old now. I'm worn down. My time to die is coming soon.

This good medicine—this excellent medicine—I'm gonna leave it right here for y'all. Y'all can take it and drink it. Don't worry that it won't heal you. It will.'

After he gave them these instructions, he left again. He went far away to another country.

Then he sent a messenger back home with a message:

'Your father is dead.'"

---

THE CHILDREN WAKE UP

"When them children heard that their father had died—that he was gone, that he'd passed away—their hearts filled up with great sorrow and pain. They thought:

'If our father was still alive, he would love us. He would protect us. He would take care of us.

But now he done left us. He done died far away in another country.

We orphans now. We ain't got nobody. We alone.'

Constant grief, constant sadness—that's what they felt. And in that grieving, in that sorrow, their minds finally came back. They woke up. They sobered up.

They realized that medicine—that medicine their father left—it actually had excellent color, excellent fragrance, excellent taste after all.

So they took it. They drank it down.

And all the poison in their bodies—it was healed. It was cured. They was completely well again.

When the father heard that all his children was healed and cured, he came back right away. He came back home and showed himself to all of them.

He let them see him alive."

---

DID THAT DOCTOR LIE? CAN YOU CALL HIM A LIAR?

"Now good sons, what you think about this?

Could anybody—could any person—say that good doctor told a lie? Could they accuse him of being false when he said he was dead?"

"No, World-Honored One. Nobody could say that."

The Buddha said:

"I'm the same way. I'm just like that doctor.

I became a Buddha countless, boundless, hundreds of thousands of millions of billions of nayutas of uncountable ages ago.

But for the sake of living beings, I use the power of skillful means and say: 'I'm about to enter nirvāṇa. I'm about to pass away.'

And ain't nobody—ain't no one anywhere—who can truthfully say I'm speaking falsely. Ain't no one can accuse me of lying.

Because what I'm doing is the Dharma. What I'm doing is the medicine. What I'm doing is truth in the form that can heal."

---

NOW THE BUDDHA GONNA SAY IT IN VERSE SO IT SINK DOWN DEEP

At that time, the World-Honored One, wanting to speak this meaning again, spoke in verse, saying:

Since I became a Buddha,

The ages I been through—

Countless hundreds of thousands of millions,

Billions, uncountable, nayutas too.

I been constantly teaching the Dharma,

Teaching countless billions of living souls,

Leading them into the Buddha-way,

Helping them reach them spiritual goals.

For countless ages it's been like this,

And to save living beings, you know,

I skillfully appear to enter nirvāṇa—

But I ain't really going, I don't really go.

I actually stay right here,

Constantly teaching this Dharma true,

I constantly dwelling in this place,

But with my spiritual power, I do—

I make the upside-down, confused beings,

Even though they close, they don't see me near.

The multitudes see me passing away,

They make offerings to my relics with many a tear.

They all cherish me and long for me,

Their hearts fill up with thirst and desire.

When living beings believe and submit like that,

When they honest and gentle, when they aspire—

When they single-minded, wanting to see the Buddha,

Not caring even if it cost their life—

Then I appear with the whole assembly

On Vulture Peak, free from all strife.

And I tell them living beings right then:

"I'm always here, I don't pass away.

It's just skillful means, the way I appear—

Sometimes here, sometimes gone—that's my way.

If beings in other countries

Are reverent, full of faith and joy,

Then I appear among them too,

Teaching the unsurpassed Dharma I employ.

But y'all don't hear about this,

Y'all just think I done passed on.

But I see all them living beings

Drowning in suffering, alone and forlorn.

So I don't show my body to them yet,

I make them long, I make them yearn.

And when their hearts full up with longing,

Then I appear—then they learn.

My spiritual power is like this,

For uncountable ages, countless time,

I been constantly on Vulture Peak

And in every other place of mine.

When living beings see the age ending,

See the world burning up in flame,

My land here—it's peaceful and calm,

It's full of gods and humans, all the same.

Gardens and groves, halls and towers,

Adorned with every kind of jewel and treasure.

Precious trees full of flowers and fruit,

Where living beings play at their leisure.

The gods strike their heavenly drums,

Always making music, never ending.

Raining down mandarava flowers,

On the Buddha and the assembly descending.

My Pure Land ain't destroyed,

But living beings see it burned to the ground.

They see worry, fear, and suffering—

All kinds of pain and trouble around.

These sinful living beings,

Because of their bad karma and evil ways,

They go through uncountable ages

Never hearing the names of the Three Jewels, all their days.

But those who practice merit and virtue,

Who gentle and honest and upright—

They all see me right here,

Teaching the Dharma in their sight.

Sometimes I tell this assembly:

'The Buddha's lifespan is measureless, without end.'

For those who only see the Buddha rarely,

I say: 'Buddhas are hard to meet, my friend.'

My wisdom power is just like this,

My light of wisdom shining without bound.

My lifespan is countless ages—

From long practice of good deeds, this life I found.

Y'all who got wisdom and intelligence,

Don't be doubtful about this teaching here.

Cut off all your doubts forever—

The Buddha's words are real and true and clear.

Like that doctor using skillful means,

To cure his crazy children's suffering plight—

He really was alive, but said he was dead,

And nobody can say that wasn't right.

I'm also the father of the world,

Saving all them suffering ones from pain.

Because ordinary people upside-down and confused,

Though I'm really here, I say I don't remain.

If they always seeing me here,

They gonna get arrogant, proud, and loose,

Selfish and lazy, attached to the five desires,

Falling down into the evil paths, no use.

I always know which living beings—

Which ones walking the path, which ones ain't.

According to what they need to hear for salvation,

I teach them all kinds of Dharma, without restraint.

Every single moment I'm thinking this:

'How can I make living beings see—

How can I help them enter the unsurpassed wisdom,

And quickly achieve the Buddha-body, free?'"

---

END OF CHAPTER SIXTEEN: HOW LONG THE TATHĀGATA'S BEEN ALIVE

---

TRANSLATION NOTES:

This chapter reveals the most profound teaching in the Lotus Sutra—that Buddha's enlightenment wasn't 40-some years ago near Gaya, but countless eons in the past. He's been here all along, teaching everywhere, appearing and disappearing like a good father who knows when his children need to see him and when they need to miss him.

The mathematics of time in this chapter defies comprehension—grinding infinite worlds to dust, walking through infinite space, dropping dust grains, then converting every world into an eon, and Buddha's been enlightened LONGER than that. It's Buddhist teaching breaking the mind's ability to calculate, forcing us past numbers into pure mystery.

The parable of the physician and his children is one of the most beautiful and troubling stories in Buddhist literature. The doctor's "lie" about his death is medicine more powerful than the herbs he left behind. His absence becomes the cure for the poison that made the good medicine look bad. This is upaya—skillful means—at its most sophisticated: truth told through apparent falsehood, healing that comes through absence rather than presence.

The closing verses reveal Buddha's eternal presence on Vulture Peak, in the Pure Land that exists right here beneath the suffering world we see with our poisoned perception. Those with eyes to see—with merit and virtue—they see gardens and jewels and heavenly music. Those still poisoned see only fire and destruction. Same place, different vision.

This is the blues truth: God's always been here, never left, but sometimes you can't see Him till you feel His absence so strong it breaks your heart open. Sometimes you gotta lose what you thought you had to find what was always there. Sometimes the truth comes dressed up like a lie because a lie dressed up like truth is all you ready to hear.

Buddha been alive since before time. Buddha gonna be alive after time done run out. And every appearance, every disappearance, every teaching, every silence—it's all medicine for the poison we been drinking since we can't remember when.

The dose is calibrated perfect for each patient. The doctor knows what he doing. Trust the medicine, even when it taste wrong. Trust the absence, even when it hurt. Trust the teaching, even when it sound like somebody playing games.

Ain't no games. It's all healing. It's all love. It's all the way home.

CHAPTER SEVENTEEN: COUNTING UP THE GRACE

(分別功德品第十七 - Distinguishing the Merits)

Blues/Gospel Translation from Kumārajīva's Classical Chinese (406 CE)

Rendered into vernacular English

THE INSTANT SALVATION

Now listen here—when the Buddha finished telling them about his eternal life, about how he been living forever and ain't never gonna die, when he laid that truth down in Chapter 16, something happened right then and there in that assembly. I mean something HAPPENED.

The World-Honored One turned to Maitreya Bodhisattva and said:

"Ajita, let me tell you what went down while I was speaking. Let me count it up for you, let me measure out the grace that fell on this congregation:

Six hundred and eighty million billion times the sands of the Ganges River—that many beings got themselves the patience that don't arise. You hear me? They reached that place where they ain't scared of nothing no more, where the truth of how things really are don't make them flinch. They got that no-fear state, that calm-in-the-storm mind.

And a thousand times that many Bodhisattvas—a THOUSAND times more—they received the dhāraṇī of perfect hearing. They can hear the Dharma once and hold it forever, won't never forget it.

And as many Bodhisattvas as there are dust particles in one whole world system—that many got themselves the gift of unobstructed eloquence. They can talk about the truth and the words just flow like water, like honey, like joy pouring out.

And as many Bodhisattvas as there are dust particles in one whole world—that many received billions and billions of dhāraṇīs, memory powers beyond counting, holding the teachings like treasure in their minds.

And as many Bodhisattvas as there are dust particles in three thousand great thousand world systems—that vast number can now turn the wheel that don't retreat. They set to rolling and ain't nothing gonna stop them, they ain't never going backward.

And as many Bodhisattvas as there are dust particles in two middle-sized world systems—that many can turn the pure Dharma wheel.

And as many Bodhisattvas as there are dust particles in one small world system—that many gonna reach supreme enlightenment within eight lifetimes. Eight births and they're home free!

And as many Bodhisattvas as there are dust particles in four continents—that many gonna make it in four lifetimes.

Three continents' worth of dust—that many in three lifetimes.

Two continents' worth—that many in two lifetimes.

One continent's worth of dust particles—that many gonna reach enlightenment in just ONE more life. One more turn and they're done!

And as many beings as there are dust particles in eight whole worlds—that vast multitude of souls set their hearts on supreme enlightenment right then and there. They made the vow, they signed up, they committed."

HEAVEN ITSELF REJOICED

Now when the Buddha finished telling about all these beings who got blessed, who received the benefit, who caught the grace—

Heaven couldn't hold it in no more.

From the empty sky came falling down mandarava flowers and great mandarava flowers, scattering over all the countless jeweled trees where Buddhas were sitting on lion thrones, scattering over the seven-jeweled stupa where Śākyamuni Buddha and long-extinct Many Treasures Tathāgata were seated together, scattering over all the great Bodhisattvas and the fourfold assembly.

And then came raining down fine powdered sandalwood and sinking-in-water aloeswood, drifting through the air like snow, like mercy, like blessing made visible.

In the empty sky, the heaven drums started playing themselves—nobody beating them, they just spoke out with wonderful sound that went deep and far, echoing through the worlds.

Then came falling a thousand kinds of heavenly garments, and strings of jewels descending—pearl necklaces, mani gem necklaces, wish-fulfilling jewel necklaces—spreading out through all nine directions.

Jeweled incense burners appeared, burning priceless incense, moving by themselves through the air, offering themselves to that great assembly.

Above every single Buddha, Bodhisattvas were holding banners and canopies, raising them up higher and higher until they reached to Brahma Heaven itself.

And those Bodhisattvas sang with wonderful voices, sang countless verses of praise to all the Buddhas.

MAITREYA SPEAKS THE WONDER

Right then Maitreya Bodhisattva rose from his seat, bared his right shoulder, pressed his palms together toward the Buddha, and spoke in verse:

*The Buddha speaks a rare teaching,*

*Something we ain't never heard before.*

*The World-Honored One got great power,*

*His life can't be measured no more.*

*Countless children of the Buddha,*

*Hearing the World-Honored One explain,*

*Telling who got what benefit—*

*Joy filling them up like rain.*

*Some standing on ground that don't retreat,*

*Some got dhāraṇīs in their mind,*

*Some got unobstructed eloquent speech,*

*Millions of total-holding of every kind.*

*Some—many as the great thousand world's dust—*

*Each one of those Bodhisattvas can turn*

*The Dharma wheel that don't go backward,*

*They rolling forward, they gonna burn.*

*And many as the middle thousand world's dust,*

*Those Bodhisattvas each can turn*

*The pure and clean Dharma wheel,*

*They got the teaching, they gonna learn.*

*And many as the small thousand world's dust,*

*Those Bodhisattvas got eight births remaining,*

*Then they gonna reach the Buddha Way,*

*They already halfway through the training.*

*And then four, three, two continents' worth,*

*That many Bodhisattvas in descending number,*

*Gonna be born that many times and reach Buddhahood—*

*They coming out they spiritual slumber.*

*One continent's dust of Bodhisattvas,*

*They got just one life left to go,*

*Then they gonna reach all-wisdom,*

*Next time around they gonna know.*

*All these beings,*

*Hearing Buddha's life is long,*

*They got immeasurable, leakless,*

*Pure fruit—everything went from wrong to strong.*

*And eight worlds' worth of dust—that many beings,*

*Hearing Buddha speak about his lifespan,*

*All of them set their hearts on the supreme,*

*They joining up with the enlightenment plan.*

*The World-Honored One speaks immeasurable truths,*

*Inconceivable teachings come out his mouth,*

*Bringing vast benefit,*

*Boundless as space—north, east, west, and south.*

*Raining down heavenly mandarava flowers,*

*And the great mandaravas too,*

*Indra and Brahma—many as Ganges sands—*

*Coming from countless Buddha-lands, coming through.*

*Raining sandalwood and aloeswood,*

*Swirling and falling in wild disarray,*

*Like birds flying down from the sky,*

*Scattering offerings on the Buddhas where they stay.*

*Heaven drums in the empty air*

*Naturally letting out wonderful sound,*

*Heavenly garments by the thousands and ten thousands*

*Spinning and descending to the ground.*

*Jeweled incense burners, wonderful and fragrant,*

*Burning priceless incense true,*

*Naturally spreading everywhere,*

*Making offerings to all the World-Honored Ones come through.*

*The great assembly of Bodhisattvas,*

*Holding seven-jeweled banners and canopies,*

*Wonderful and high—millions and billions of kinds—*

*Raising them in sequence up to Brahma's boundaries.*

*Before each and every Buddha,*

*Jeweled poles hanging victory banners down,*

*And with thousands and ten thousands of verses*

*They singing the Tathāgatas' renown.*

*All these various things,*

*Never happened before like this,*

*Hearing Buddha's life is limitless,*

*Everybody filled with bliss.*

*The Buddha's name heard in the ten directions,*

*Widely benefiting all living beings,*

*Everyone equipped with good roots,*

*Helping them toward the supreme, toward what enlightenment means.*

THE SHOCKING COMPARISON

Right then the Buddha told Maitreya Bodhisattva:

"Ajita, listen now. If there's beings who hear about the Buddha's lifespan being long like I just explained—if they can, even for one single thought-moment, generate faith and understanding—the merit they gonna get, that merit ain't got no limit. It can't be measured.

Now suppose there's a good man or a good woman who wants supreme perfect enlightenment, and for that goal they practice the Five Perfections for eighty myriads of millions of billions of nayutas of eons—

Let me tell you what that looks like:

GIVING: For all those eons, giving gifts. Giving to Buddhas, giving to solitary realizers, giving to Bodhisattvas. Giving precious and wonderful food and drink. Giving the finest clothes and bedding. Building temples out of sandalwood. Creating parks and gardens to make them beautiful. All kinds of giving, every kind wonderful and fine, through all those countless eons, dedicating it all toward the Buddha Way.

KEEPING PRECEPTS: Pure ethical conduct, clean and without a crack or leak, seeking the supreme path, the kind of conduct all Buddhas praise.

PATIENCE: Practicing forbearance, dwelling in that gentle, flexible ground. Even when all kinds of evil people come attacking, adding insults and harm, the mind don't tilt, don't tip over, don't move. Even when people who got a little bit of the Dharma come at you with their arrogance, looking down on you and troubling you—you can bear that too.

DILIGENT EFFORT: Vigorously progressing, your intention and mindfulness always solid and firm, for immeasurable hundreds of millions of eons, single-minded, never resting, never slacking off even once.

MEDITATION: Living for countless eons in quiet, empty places, whether sitting or walking, always—except when sleeping—keeping your mind collected and controlled. And because of this, producing all the meditations and concentrations, stable and unshaken in mind for eighty hundreds of millions of eons.

Taking all this one-pointed meditative merit and using it to seek the supreme path. Getting all the wisdom, mastering all the meditation states.

For hundreds and thousands of millions and billions of eons, this person practices all these meritorious virtues just like I described.

But then—

If there's a good man or good woman who hears me speak about my lifespan and believes it for even ONE SINGLE THOUGHT-MOMENT—

The blessing they get EXCEEDS all that other merit.

It ain't even close.

Not one part in a hundred. Not one part in a thousand. Not one part in hundreds of thousands of millions of billions. Can't even calculate the comparison. Can't make the numbers work. The metaphors fail.

If a good man or good woman has this kind of merit from believing—

Impossible they gonna retreat from supreme enlightenment.

Can't happen.

No such place exists where that could occur."

THE BUDDHA SPEAKS IT IN VERSE

Then the World-Honored One, wanting to speak this meaning again, said in verse:

*If someone seeking Buddha wisdom*

*For eighty myriads of millions of billions*

*Of nayuta eons*

*Practices the Five Perfections—*

*During all those eons,*

*Giving offerings to Buddhas,*

*To solitary realizers and their disciples,*

*And to Bodhisattva assemblies—*

*Precious and wonderful food and drink,*

*Fine clothing and bedding,*

*Building sandalwood temples,*

*Decorating them with gardens and groves—*

*All this kind of giving,*

*Various and wonderful in every way,*

*Through all those eons,*

*Dedicating it to the Buddha Way—*

*If they also keep the precepts,*

*Pure and clean, without crack or leak,*

*Seeking the supreme path,*

*The kind all Buddhas praise and speak—*

*If they also practice patience,*

*Dwelling on that ground of gentle adjustment,*

*Even when crowds of evil come attacking,*

*Their mind don't incline, don't make that judgment—*

*And when those who got a little Dharma,*

*Holding on to their prideful ways,*

*Look down and trouble this patient one,*

*Still able to endure throughout their days—*

*If they also diligently progress,*

*Their will and mindfulness constantly firm and strong,*

*For immeasurable hundreds of millions of eons*

*Single-minded, never lazy for long—*

*And for countless eons more,*

*Dwelling in quiet and empty places,*

*Whether sitting or walking,*

*Except for sleep, always gathering their mental spaces—*

*And because of this,*

*Able to produce all those meditation states,*

*For eighty hundreds of millions of eons*

*Dwelling in mental stability, mind that concentrates—*

*Holding this one-pointed blessing,*

*Seeking the supreme Way in their heart,*

*Saying: 'I will get all-wisdom,*

*Exhaust all meditative arts'—*

*This person, for hundreds and thousands*

*And millions and billions of eons,*

*Practices all these meritorious virtues*

*Like I just described with all these reasons—*

*But if there's good men or women*

*Who hear me speak about my lifespan,*

*And believe it even for one thought-moment,*

*Their blessing exceeds that other plan.*

*If a person completely without*

*Any doubt or regret at all,*

*Believes with deep mind even briefly,*

*Their blessing is like this—so tall.*

*Those Bodhisattvas who,*

*For immeasurable eons, walked the Way,*

*Hearing me speak about my lifespan,*

*They can believe and accept what I say.*

*People like this*

*Receive this scripture on the crown of their head,*

*Making this vow: 'In the future,*

*May I have long life and leave none unfed.*

*Just like the World-Honored One today,*

*King among the Shakya clan,*

*Lion-roaring in the place of enlightenment,*

*Speaking Dharma fearless—that's my plan.*

*We, in the worlds to come,*

*Honored and respected by everyone there,*

*When sitting in the place of enlightenment,*

*Will speak about lifespan in the same way—with care.'*

*If there are those with deep mind,*

*Pure and straightforward and true,*

*Much learning and able to retain it,*
